RATING: 7
A race game where the goal is to be the first to collect a set number of rubies, while travelling/route-planning on a modular board.

On your turn, you move your stack of pieces in a limited fashion (orthogonal, and up to 2 spaces) and either collect a piece you left behind on a tile and perform the action of that tile/location or leave a piece of your stack behind and perform the action (this movement is very similar to Dorn's Traders of Genoa).  There are several different tiles with different actions but they generally consist of: a) getting more resources to put in your wagon, getting money, acquiring rubies (which generally require expenditure of money and/or resources), and acquiring abilities/benefits (such as an upgraded wagon, special powers).  There are a few characters which you may encounter on your travels and you can interact with them for a small fee resulting in a small bonus.

This game is relatively simplistic even though it is somewhat interesting.  Having played the base game and the expansion, I wouldn't go back without the expansion as it adds some more options/paths to a game that's pretty limited decision-wise.  I'm a bit surprised for the love this game is getting, even as a hardcore Rudiger Dorn fan, I'd rate this about his 5th best game.  Only played as a 2er, but it works very well at that count, which is a real plus.  Very nice components.

Own the [boardgame=172505]Mocha & Baksheesh[/boardgame] and the [boardgame=192779]Brief & Siegel[/boardgame] expansions and the [boardgame=161224]Kebab Shop[/boardgame] spielbox promo.

RATING: 8.5
This one is well known perhaps (certainly is around BGG) and I had high expectations. It certainly did not disappoint! The mechanics are very smart - move your stack, drop/pick up a disk to take the target action. Pay other players if they are already there. And to spice it up a bit, have special pieces to move around (and capture). There are 16 location boards that can be arrange in countless ways - so a level of replay-ability for sure. After that, alot of standard Euro goodness: 5 different resources, convert to money and then to rubies (the objective of the game is to get 5 rubies). It allows for a variety of plans to be made, paths to be taken - and just a touch of random luck (a few locations use dice) and a dash of confrontation (you can get in other's ways).

Anyhow, very deserving I think of the Spiel gamer's award. Very happy to have this one and will be anxious to play.

RATING: 6
Year 2014 seems to be the year of worker movement games. I quite like the mechanic of moving your worker in a grid of action spaces. This really forces you to plan ahead to gain synergy from your moves.

However, there is one thing that I am not so fond of. This is the abrupt game ending. First, all players spend several rounds gathering speed, then everyone makes the final rush for diamonds which lasts for 3-4 rounds and then... game over. It is just my personal opinion but I rather scored points along the way working towards the final climax. I want to make decisions whether to score points now or risk my chances and try to score big in the end.

Anyways, solid mechanics and fun "plan ahead to synergise" type of game. Refreshing after classical worker placement and engine building games, I would say.

RATING: 6
*Rating based on one play.

I'd heard a lot of buzz about this game, but I found it to be a bit lackluster.  I feel like the way the game works with leaving your assistants behind on locations you visit encourages players to work out an optimized circuit of 3-5 tiles and simply visit them over and over again, which doesn't make for interesting play.  Granted I have only one play to base this observation on and so I could be completely wrong, but the game didn't really excite me in any other aspect to motivate me to test out my theory further.

I can't really put my finger on why it fell flat for me (beyond seeming to encourage repetitive strategies).  It has a lot of things I normally like in games: resource management, limited randomness, multiple paths to victory, some engine building and power comboing.  One thing that did sour me was the fact that I could see the game was won 2-3 turns in advance, and there was nothing I could do to make up the gap at that point.  It removed any suspense from the end game.

In the end, I guess I just didn't find the game's unique characteristic (the movement mechanics) interesting, so there was nothing to set the game apart from the dozens of other Euros I've played.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Not Constantinople is great game. The board is modular and has several configuration options to keep things interesting. The premise is that you are a merchant trying to collect a certain number of rubies first. You do so by moving around the board and triggering specific actions where you drop off subordinates, or as I like to call it, "pooping" on various spots of the board. This innovative pooping mechanic forces you to be efficient with the actions you choose because when you run out of poops you have to either go back to a spot where you left a turd or waste a turn activating a tile that allows you to collect all your poops back. The idea is to be as efficient as possible in executing various pick up and delivers as possible while creating synergies between certain board tiles so that you are minimizing your need to waste actions. Istanbul is a solid game... Poop.

RATING: 7
I played this game just once, so these are mostly impressions that may change after multiple plays.

Even if I really enjoy euro-games, for some reason I never really pulled the trigger on this one. Now that I finally got a chance to play it, I can see how Istanbul is a really solid euro-style game and one that certainly deserves the following that it got. 

Everything about this production is really top-notch. The cardboard is nice and thick, the artwork is great, the components are high quality. All together the game is very visually appealing... and, as always with euro-games, Istanbul comes with an interesting and deep game-play.

I really like its base concept. It takes a different spin on the worker-placement genre, by mixing in a rondel-like mechanism and a very intriguing way of moving your pieces around. The rules are simple, but the game-play is very deep and challenging. You really need to figure out how to be the most efficient with the way you move your workers on the board. It's such a fascinating puzzle!

Yet, while I liked the game and I appreciate the design, Istanbul didn't excite me. I cannot even really point at what I would change to improve my experience with the game. I just think it's ultimately a bit seamy and it doesn't stand out enough from the crowd of the euro-game genre. Maybe I'll change my mind with future plays.

To sup up... I like Istanbul and I would definitively play it again. I can see how being more familiar with the mechanisms and the tactics of the game could make me like it more. It's just not one of those games that I keep thinking about when I'm done playing it. It's good... but not great, at least for me.

RATING: 8
The action selection mechanism is great and the game plays smoothly. The game end and victory condition is refreshingly different than what’s usually expected in these kinds of games. The game end can be AP prone since everyone is trying to find a way to finish the game. The game works better with 4 than 5 players, especially if the starting place is in the corner. In a 5-player game it can be that the last player has to follow a path someone else is already choosing and he has to donate his starting money to the other player. This problem could have been nicely solved by giving one more coin for each player in the beginning. This way the fifth player would have 7 coins and he could pursuit for the extra storage right in the beginning.

RATING: 8
Istanbul is a fairly easy grid moving game with a modular board. The task is simple: get to 5 (or 6) gems as fast as possible. The challeging part is that you have to leave one of your discs behind each time you move, and you need a disc to perform actions. You therefore have to carrefully plan every move, in order to get your discs back. Not the best game ever, but certainly very enjoyable to play, especially with newcomers since it is easy to understand and play

 This one clearly ranks as one of our favorite Euros. A worker placement game with simple rules and just enough strategy to keep you thinking from the first move to the last. You move about Istanbul (was Constantinople!) collecting wares and selling them or using them to buy the rubies you need to win the game. Along the way you gamble, have your near do well family go out & do some business for you and even visit a beautiful fountain. The production is top notch and the theme is fairly strong if off-putting to some. We love it a lot. We are really hoping an expansion or two might come out because that will make this already great game a perfect 10 possibly.

RATING: 7
Played demo at GenCon 2014.

Very interesting and reminds me of Trade of Genoa (without the negotiation) meets Blue Moon City.

You collect goods, exchange them for money, which you then in turn exchange for victory points (completing wheelbarrow, completing add-ons, changing resources/money into victory points).

The escalating cost of exchanging into victory points is very similar to Blue Moon City and causes a very anxious game where you have to out-race other people.

Update: Played a second time and it kinda fell flat.  What was interesting the first game became repetitive in the second.  Downgrading it to a 7, possibly a 6. .

RATING: 9
A very unique puzzle that plays really smoothly and surprisingly fast. The game is about being efficient with your movements and finding a circuit that gains you resources and money, while picking your workers back up. There are many paths to victory. Players can have very different strategies and still be neck and neck at the end. Gameplay matches the theme really well, and the artwork is delightful.

The only thing I dislike is that near the end of the game, players can see who has won and how many turns it will take for him to finish. For that reason, it can be anticlimatic as players helplessly watch someone win when they can't do anything about it.

RATING: 5
This is a medium-weight race game with resource management, push-your-luck actions, and a unique logistic/abstract puzzle where players must drop/pick-up workers to do actions; If you don't plan ahead, you may not be able to do actions or do them as efficiently. 

That being said, I am hesitant to call this game an "euro." Many things (Dice rolling, Card Drawing) can swing the game significantly and the variance/influence of the randomizers are not well controlled. The game essentially plays like an elaborate push-your-luck game. 

Is it bad? For those that enjoy the gambling tension that dice and powerful cards can bring... this may provide an enjoyable experience. But for euro-gamers that detest their decisions being mitigated/limited by dumb luck, I can see this having a short life. That being said, the game is relatively simple and plays quite fast... as long as you don't take it too seriously, it may prove enjoyable.

RATING: 9
Winner of the Kennerspiel de Jahres (2014).

Fantastic game, elegant and breezy. Plays quickly, so the only negative - you can usually see defeat coming a turn or two before the game actually ends - is mitigated by the speed of replay. Pre-set or randomized layouts mean a Dominion-like replayability. Game strategy mostly consists of finding best routes for a given arrangement and how you might disrupt someone else's patterns.

Lots of fun, and its quick and easy replay means multiple games per session.

RATING: 7
Istanbul is a neat and polished efficiency game.

Players move their merchants plus assistants' stacks on a 4x4 grid, performing the action of the tile on which they arrive (only if they can add or remove an assistant to/from the stack).

Every tile in the grid has its own special action, usually concerning acquisition/selling of wares or money.

There are a couple of more twists related to family members, smuggler and governor.

The goal is being first to acquire 5 (or 6 in a two player game) rubies.

A well deserved Kennerspiel des Jahers, I am keen to explore and try with different numbers of players.

RATING: 7
Ostensibly a market/trading game, but really more like a tense, quickfire race game in which you have to plot out the most efficient route to grab five rubies before your opponents. It's kind of like a worker placement game, but the placement requires more spatial thinking and you can't block other players, just hinder them slightly on their way. My favourite thing about Istanbul is that, after an initial infodump explaining what all the locations do, the game is incredibly intuitive and easy to pick up, and it plays super quickly (the 40-60 minutes on the box is accurate).

RATING: 9
Every once in a while there is a game that, for no reason you can clearly explain, just clicks with you. I wouldn't normally describe myself as a fan of games that are mechanical exercises; where you do this to get that, and use that to get the other thing that's worth points. It's not that I hate them, I just usually think of myself as someone who enjoys a good theme over a good mechanism. But Istanbul makes so much sense once you get over the hurdle of learning the game, and I love thinking 3 to 4 turns in advance to plan out how you will get points. This is a game that I am afraid I will struggle to get people interested in, but I will want to play all the time. Definitely one I love, and will recommend to others.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
An enjoyable pick-up-and-deliver game with a bit of worker placement, only one action potter turn and a fairly unique action restriction mechanism involving a stack of discs.  The disc stack represents your potential since you must leave a disc (apprentice) behind each time you perform a building tile's action.  Once all of your discs are out on the board, you need to go back and pick them up (relating those actions if you can) or spend a turn (or more) moving to the fountain building and calling your discs back to you, unless you have a special ability (e.g., bonus time) allowing you to reclaim discs another way.  You can only perform a few actions before getting overextended, then you need to reclaim discs in order to continue performing actions, meaning that you need to repeat certain actions or spend a turn calling your discs (apprentices) back to you; this makes the game tricky, challenging and fun.  Finding an efficient route/loop is very important, but other players may (and should) interfere too stop you from exploiting that route to the fullest... thus preventing an easy win.  The modular board will make each game different from the last, but over time, the base building actions may grow boring.  That said, I suspect that printable fan-made or official expansion buildings will emerge to spice it up.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
First of all, Istanbul is a racing game! Whoever gets 5 gems first, wins the game... and usually racing games are not my cup of tea.

But this one is good! The artwork is stunning, colorful tiles (the board), with good looking red gems.

The gameplay is fluid, the turns don't take too long to execute, and since you do the action of the place where you have landed on, there's not much to think about. Very intuitive and elegant gameplay.

The family members and the penalty for merchant encounters creates some interesting interaction.

There's a variety of ways to get gems. But I feel, in some board setups, the first player has some advantage 'cause there are ideal/obvious choices that can put you ahead of the race, early in the game. Experienced players will take it, for sure.

But since there some pre-set setups, it adds replayability and strategy changes. You can randomize it if you want to, although it's not recommended.

It got a couple of awards and it's easy to understand why!

The length it's just fine!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
- great strategy game where the winner is the first person who find the way to gather 5 rubies (6 for 2 players)
- it is not just about planning, it is also fight with time
- at each location in the game (16), you can carry out a specific action
- you must move your merchant and an assistant there, then leave the assistant behind. If you want to use that assistant again later, your merchant must return to that location to pick him up. You don't want to stay without them because merchant can't do actions alone.. Mechanics many can know from "Genua" & "Goa" ;)
- it is kind of the game where one bad decision can cost player a loss
- game has strong theme, You can feel it while playing
- playing time (40-75 minutes)
- I had to change dices (basic, white) in the game - wanted to add some Istanbul theme to it.

5 players* - it is more chaotic but still awesome. I enjoyed the 5 players game the same as head to head.

STRONG RECOMMENDATION.

Other games created by Rudiger Dorn worth look at: GOA (my rate - 9), GENUA

But if You are looking for the game for 2 players only (with a  great theme too) try "TARGI" instead of this one.

**I have Kebab Shop mini expansion.

RATING: 7.8
Un juego sencillito, casi familiar, en el que el tablero se forma con una serie de losetas (16 losetas) sobre las que los jugadores se pueden jugar con mercaderes y una serie de ayudantes. Van todos en pila. Cuando llegan a una loseta, para usar la acción relacionada, debe dejarse un ayudante. OTra posibilidad es recoger un ayudante en una loseta de destino. Eso le da un poco que pensar. El objetivo es ser el primero en conseguir 5 gemas. Cada loseta te permite obtener una serie de recursos que se pueden luego usar en otras acciones, para conseguir las preciadas Gemas. 

Está divertido. Es entretenido y tiene muy buena producción. Apto, e incluso recomendable, para no jugones.

RATING: 9
Quick additional comment: I like this game a lot at the 45-60 minutes advertised play time (which is what I see the vast majority of the time). But it can be a possible AP trap, and if the game goes 90 minutes it's at best a 6-rated game for me. I don't think it's the game's fault in this case; players need to play in a timely manner. But the potential is there, if you have a problem player.

---

This is an excellent Rüdiger Dorn game that I think I would have absolutely loved as recently as maybe 5-7 years ago, but I now I find that while I like it and think its very good and I recommend it to most gamers, it just doesn't quite grab me. The generic "medieval traders" has just been done so completely to death at this point that I find I miss the nice world-building of Blue Moon City or the high interactivity of Traders of Genoa, just to pick a couple games that are in the same sort of ballpark of special-powers-plus-money-management. I think this is in the category of "it's not you, it's me" though; Istanbul really is an excellent, first-class game which I enjoy playing, but for me personally I'm now 20+ years into the German game phenomenon and this type of game is fine but is never going to win Chris' personal game of the year. It's just too much like too many other games that I have spent too much time playing.

RATING: N/A
Okay game nothing to get excited about
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A really neat route optimization game, where the "hook" is that you have a dude that moves around with his posse to various tiles that allow you to do different things.  In order to do stuff at a tile, you either need to drop a member of your posse, or be rejoining a member of your posse (that you dropped off there in a previous turn).  So, you can't just run around will nilly, as spreading your posse all over the place leaves you with very few options.

However, after a few games, the game quickly started feeling repetitive. Yes, there's random set-up where you can see different optimal paths from game to game, but it still felt like I was doing mostly the same things, so the game flamed out for me prettyy quickly.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I have played five times, and have won all five times, using the same basic strategy.  I am convinced that this strategy dominates all other strategies independent of board layout so long as other players do not attempt to interfere.  I do not expand my wheelbarrow or frequent either of the mosques, preferring instead to use the Post Office, Black Market, Sultan's Palace, and Gemstone Dealer almost exclusively, with stops in the Warehouses and Caravansary as needed.  Depending on board layout, this approach ranges from marginal (second place player is within two actions of a win) to devastating (second place players had only two gems).  Does this mean the game is broken?  No, because if other players adopt the same strategy, it will reduce its efficiency to the point where other strategies (i.e., Wainwright and Mosque) will likely succeed.  It will be interesting to see if the gameplay of Istanbul evolves or stagnates over time.

RATING: 3
UPDATE 8/16: Played it again and ... still no. Is it fair to look down on this game because I like another (thematically similar, mechanically very different) game by the same designer way more? No? Screw it, I'm doing it anyway. This game is not fit to wash Genoa's jockstrap. It's an efficiency-over-actions puzzle/race without the slightest hint of meaningful player interaction. I'm capable of liking games with that profile -- I like Le Havre ferchrissake -- but I usually find this sort of thing, well, boring. You may well like it. Lots of smart people with taste do, and there's no question it does what it sets out to do, I just don't care for what it sets out to do.

--

The Crunchy Frog of board games. Dew-picked and flown in directly from Iraq, lightly killed, lovingly frosted with glucose, the whole nine, and all that's as may be but it's still a frog.

(And by "frog," in this case I mean "generic puzzly cube-pushing Euro with no discernible theme and minimal interaction.")

Istanbul does what it sets out to do and does it in a reasonable time frame, I just feel like I've seen everything it sets out to do before, and I would rather put my time to more engaging ends.

RATING: 8
Fusm
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
It's nice to see Rüdiger Dorn return to his 'breadcrumbs' mechanism. In this particular case the breadcrumbs can be re-absorbed into the main draw pile creating more of an accordion-like (caterpillar-like?) effect that stretches and contracts across the board... although with a recall part of the charm of this analogy is lost.

Of course what the players are after is nothing special and worthy of comment. Collect resources here, bring them over there, transform them into rubies/VP, race to the end. All you need to know further is what is done where and how actions are effected, no special remarkable features here. In the first game I played I was unpleasantly surprised by the inherent luck in the 2D6 rolls which are barely mitigated, causing me to wonder why people were allowed to receive expensive blue goods or a nice amount of spare pocket money for nearly free. However, I was at the time unaware of the effect of the mosque tiles (especially the tile which gives you a random good for 2 lira if you stock up), and also not really making use of the bonus cards. Both are sufficiently powerful to avoid the use of the dice altogether, or at least make you stock up in what appears to be a reasonably equal tempo. So in that the game is more balanced than I initially assumed. Still, I don't think it is a Good Idea to include such raw dice mechanisms in games in this day and age: we've spent a decade working out how to do better with dice, and all that knowledge is blatantly thrown out the window here.

The above grumbling aside, the game itself purrs along without too much problems. At the lower end of the player scale people rarely need interfere with each other, although I have to say I found the mechanism to send someone else's family member back to prison to be nastier than I thought: if you place the family member in a spot where noone ever visits, then you won't get him back all that easily either.

In the end, I think that what is going to end up happening is the following: there are not many ways in which you get the rubies, and the corresponding prerequisites do not change either. So you'll be walking similar paths over and over again, with the winner being the player who traced out the most efficient path. The only variation is that some paths are at times longer, and at times shorter. I'm not saying that spotting the correct path is easy, so the game tends to promote a bit of 'mucking about' and 'learning by doing' every time. I doubt this is a pattern you'll keep on coming back to again and again after you've played it a few times. And that raises questions about who this game is acutally for. There are quite a few rules to make it fall under the family game-aegis; yet the patterns it creates are not, in my opinion, sufficiently interesting to draw in enthusiast gamers. I have decided to increase my initial rate by a point to reflect my increased understanding of how all elements work together... but it's going to stay put at this number for some time to come, I fear.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
The game plays pretty easy and fast, there's little down time. It takes a few turns before you know all the tiles, but then it's fairly straightforward. You gather goods, sell these for money and buy rubies or instead deliver them to the sultan for rubies.

The Sultan's route is a bit more complex, as you'll need blue goods (jewelry) as well, which cannot be gathered at the warehouses, but need to be won by throwing dice (7 or up gets you one or more Jewels). 

People who usually are unlucky with dice can avoid this by acquiring a card that lets you buy jewely at the warehouses or a different card that allows you to change one die of throw again.

As you need to leave your assistants behind (to take care of the paper work or something), you need to plan your route carefully. Luckily, you can always return to the fountain and recall your assistants there.

The fun part is that you encounter people on your way, other Merchants, the governour, the smuggler or one of the "nephews", either your own or someone elses. The other Merchants have to be paid off before they allow you to perform the action, the governour and the smuggler allow you to buy cards or goods and the nephews can be sent to jail for a reward. You can even turn in your own kin. 

All in all, Rudiger Dorn has put together another nice game. I see some resemblance to one of his earlier games Waka Waka, where the same goods (fruits, spices and silk) can be gathered and delivered for more status. We like that game as well, but this one is certainly more developed. 

As this is a modular board (all the 16 tiles of the market can be set up randomly), the most efficient way of moving around varies per game, so replayability is high. There's a nice alternative play as well, where one of your assistants has a neutral colour and can therefore be taken by one of your opponents.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Simpler than average, quicker than average resource collection / conversion game. Play this game rather than one of many other similar games if planning movements so as to minimize "wasted" movements that replenish your forces sounds like fun. That aspect of the game reminds me of the bumping feature of Euphoria. In Euphoria, you hope to be bumped so that you can postpone retrieval; in Istanbul, you want to re-visit spaces so that you can postpone re-stacking.

RATING: 8
Players are merchants, carting goods around Istanbul in a wheelbarrow.  Simple route planning game, where players move their stack of Merchant + Assistants one or two spaces around a 4x4 modular game board.  They then deploy/pick up an assistant there to take the space's action.  Actions are a nice mix of accumulating goods, cash, or converting stuff into rubies, which is what you need to win the game.

Very nice game, well designed, moves along well, fast playing time - almost a super filler, feels very old school and thank goodness, there is no worker placement.  Nice variety of strategies - and I love the fact you can try your luck with dice, or ignore that and go a more controlled route.  Certainly feels like a Dorn game - touches of Goa, Louis XIV, Traders of Genoa, etc.  Very impressed, liked it a lot.

RATING: N/A
[b][FirstImpression/PlayOnApril8th2014][/b] Finally get to play this game! I've had much anticipation when I first saw this game during Essen'13 demo. I have to admit, I was super excited about the game when I look at the components. Call it love at first sight. I also happen to like some of Rudiger Dorn's designs.

It's a simple and light game, and I like that it can handle up to 5 players. People who are familiar with Rudiger Dorn's design will feel somewhat at home when you see how the merchant and assistants move. I like how the mechanic of the Post Office board works (take four revealed icons and slide one down) as well as the Tea House (name a value and roll to see if you get the money). How the gems become expensive after each purchase is also neat as with each new purchase, you'll reveal a new cost/good needed for the next gem purchase.

This is not a deep game but I love the simplicity. It's right up my alley.

[b]Status: Purchased on 10th AUGUST 2014[/b]
[b]Status: Sold, September 2015[/b]

After 1 Play: Fun pick up and deliver type game that involves making sure you can backtrack to fulfill certain spaces in order to pick up gemstones. RATING: 7

It is also rather simple and has that charming board game feel. I do wonder whether a human calculator could solve out the optimal move on any given turn however, it is rather deterministic and the player interaction is relatively low.

RATING: 6
Istanbul is a pretty quick efficiency euro. It has a modular board which helps with the replay value, but has been criticized as having some uneven setups. I would recommend using [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/thread/1583507/balanced-place-tile-layout-german-board-game-champ]these rules[/url] if you aren't playing with an expansion. The basic idea of the game is to optimize your restricted movements on the board to take actions which will allow you to get gems. You can trade coins or goods for gems. You can also get gems via upgrading yourself with storage capacity or special abilities. The game is quick, attractive, and pretty easy to understand. In addition, people can feel competitive as it's a race to get 5 gems, which people generally stay pretty close to one another. With all that being said, I don't think there would be enough replay value for me to keep this one without playing with at least one of the expansions.

RATING: 6
[1 play] This was a decently enjoyable light game. The most interesting thing to me was the mechanic of moving your stack around, dropping off and picking up workers. Apparently the designer (Rudiger Dorn) has done similar stuff in other games that I must not have played. But one of the players in my game opined that this wouldn’t have staying power, and I agree. This doesn’t seem like a game we’ll be playing in the years to come. One negative to me is that the scoring seems insufficiently granular. I won the game, the player after me would have won on her turn, and everyone else also had 4 rubies. It seemed likely to always be like that. Of course I allow the possibility that I haven’t gotten everything out of this game in one try, but that’s how it struck me. In the short term, I would be happy to play again but won’t intently seek it out.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A perfectly playable middle weight euro in the quintessential sense. You scramble up tiles that offer actions and lay them out as the de facto board. Then you figure out an optimal path to take and try to adhere to it as much as possible but then people get in your way and start driving the cost up. 

Every time you move you have to drop a worker, so at some point you have to rewind to get your stack of workers back in your hand while hitting actions in an order that benefits you. The ostensible fun is doing that better than your opponents. It's logistics and action chaining efficiency; either that works for you or else it'll seem dull with a pasted on theme. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

RATING: N/A
I love, love, love:
- the race-style gameplay
- the mechanism of having a "leash" of workers that limits how far you can go on the board
- that the rules include an intro set-up

I dislike:
- that the setting is on an all-male extraterrestrial Earth-like planet
- that some of the places can really be combo-spammed over and over if a player is left unchecked
- that the game starts to seem too easy(?) once you know what you're doing
- whatever shady thing is going on at the post office

Final verdict: Love this clever mechanism, and I'll most likely be getting an expansion to add more places to visit/use.

RATING: 6.5
Rating based on 2 plays (1x5 players - easy board, 1x4 players - long board).  Lightweight game that is a good intro  to Euro style games and which plays fairly quickly - 45 to 60 minutes.   There is little "development" during the game, so once an optimum strategy is found for the board "as is" (given that the board is variable) then that is pretty much it. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I really like the mancala-esque mechanic of moving your merchant and his followers around. You need to plan several turns in advance of where you would like to end up so that each turn you get to do the action of the space you land on (either because you leave a follower behind or you are picking one up) Missing out on an action because of the lack of a follower to drop off/pick up leaves you at quite the disadvantage.

you race around the board hoping to be the first to collect the required number of rubies which can be collected for various different actions.  The board being modular as well means set up will be different each time and thus strategy will change dependent upon this set up. 

The action spaces are simple and clear and the iconography very good. I like the subdued colour scheme.  I was not expecting much from this when I entered the game but I was very pleasantly surprised and really really enjoyed this one.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I quite enjoy this game.  The board randomizes every time, which helps with replayability.  However, it has two major downsides to me.  First, every time I play, it seems as though someone else win right when I feel as though I've got momentum going.  Winner is the first to collect five rubies (6 in a 2 player game).  I feel like it should be first to collect 10.  That may just be my play style working against me though.

The second downside is that, IMO, in a game with 4-5 players, the player who goes last, despite starting with extra coins, gets a significant disadvantage.  The game discourages them from going in a square any other player is in, and there's only a few squares that are actually useful to go to in the beginning few turns.  They're almost always occupied by other characters when it gets to the last player's turn.

RATING: 5
Istanbul is a Dorn game so, quite unsurprisingly, you get another implementation of his hallmark breadcrumbs mechanism here. Unfortunately, the game beyond those breadcrumbs is rather flat. If you look at some of his older designs (e.g. Louis XIV or Goa) you could claim that they mostly consisted of said breadcrumbs as well but in those titles, the game they supported was fairly tight. Each move you made mattered. Istanbul, on the other hand, is wide open. The spatial puzzle is not entirely without merit but the game is still just about squeezing a little more efficiency out of your moves than the competition. There are no big, decisive moves to be had. And in this light, breadcrumbs alone make for a rather paltry meal.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Some ideas are neat and the gameplay matches the theme fairly well. I really liked the concept behind Istanbul while reading the rules. However, I had three big problems. Firstly, there are too many rules. I had to refer to them during our first game all the time. Secondly, I found that the coolest idea was that we had to leave our helpers behind and then find a way to get them. This gave a spatial element to the game tactics. But not enough for my taste: the board was a bit too small. It felt like they had this really cool idea but they also wanted to add a worker placement edge to the whole thing. So a large emphasis was on the variety of the actions at different locations. However, I am not a fan of worker placement, so this fell flat for me. Thirdly, there was almost no player interaction. There was some through a quasi supply-demand mechanism and we had to sometimes pay each other, but it was not enough for me.

All in all: Definitely not a bad game and I want to give it a try again sometime. It has some ideas I really like, but there were too little emphasis on them. Will definitely keep an eye on the designer.

RATING: 8
I thought Istanbul would feel too much like recycled mechanics from other Dorn games, but I was quite wrong! Yes, it uses a similar "tower" mechanic as found in Genoa and Goa, but that's largely where the similarities end. Istanbul takes that mechanic and then uses a very simple quasi-worker placement system to gather resources and coins in order to buy jewels. But there are 16 different actions, some only variations of others and some that are unique and interesting! I love the prison and the action where you can gamble for a payout (I forget the name of the building). Just little extra things like that which help this game shine. In addition to the regular "gain resources to buy jewels" there are additional bonus actions and powers that players can gain, and these also really add to the experience. Perhaps this game doesn't feel as innovative as Goa, but it's a tight, solid game and certainly one of Dorn's best.

RATING: 7.8
We warmed up the gaming day with Istanbul, the Spiel des Jahres winning from 2014. Playing it, it isn't surprising, its a solid and clever design that is elegant with a veneer of complexity that isn't too deep as to scare away families. I like the trend of Euros with better and more interesting themes and this one is dripping with it more than usually. The theme masks all the abstract mechanisms well, I still found myself wondering how you swap fruits for rubies and how to do it in real life. But it made sense that you wheelbarrow around the city with assistants to do stuff for you. I enjoy the spatial elements of traversing the city and its one of my favorite race games. And a race it is.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A very strong design, with finely tuned mechanisms. The tradeoffs of executing a useful action, setting up for movement across the board, and benefits from encounters is very well done. In particular, I like the scope of the 4x4 board, with the max movement of 2 spaces. It creates a very interesting dynamic in accordance with the layout of the particular actions. The addition of family members, with the immediate upside to you plus the potential risk if caught by another player, is inspired.

I also appreciate the generally positive nature of the design. For example, it would have been very easy for the Governor and Smuggler mechanisms to have been more "screw you", for example, blocking access to a location rather than presenting an (optional) opportunity with what feels like a very reasonable cost.

Finally, the fact that you don't have to worry too much about the other players and can generally focus on your own strategy, knowing that there may just be some additional marginal cost due to the others, is a great strength, especially for newer players.

Overall, the game is just very well done, and certainly deserves the accolades it received.

RATING: 6.6
The Designer picked up an idea from one of his earlier games (Louis IVX) concerning workerplacement, made a variant and added the usual stuff like collecting resources to turn them into victory points aka Rubies.
A solid design, nice components, however it did not feel as fresh as I hoped. Also it is low on interaction, you don't care that much what others do unless they occupy a space you want to go to but want also save the two money you would had to pay. At the heart of the game, it's an abstract one: you optimize paths on a 4x4 grid. Theme is exchangeable, you could be as well be collecting buckets full of water to extinguish a fire. That's all - ok game but not impressive. Maybe I played too many games yet...

RATING: 6.5
Resource conversion game with an odd way of action selection. Moving over a grid of locations, each allowing a different action, reminded a bit of "Royal Palace". However, you are limited not only by distance, but also by the discs that you drop and pick up again along the way. This, unfortunately, rewards repeating actions instead of variety. The game is a totally abstract race to acquire five rubies using a few different methods, but all involve buying upgrades or the rubies themselves with goods or money. Everything works, but two actions unnecessarily rely on dice rolls and I felt that luck can be decisive.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game seems like a cross between Blue Moon City (because of the 1-2 space orthogonal movement and the "5 rubies to win" rule) and a worker placement/resource collection game like Agricola.  

I liked it a lot on my first play, and would like to play it more.  It's interesting that I like it, because I usually dislike games that use dice, but this game doesn't hinge too much on the dice rolls -- they're just a way to add some randomness.

With 5 players, there is a good amount of fun competition for certain actions on the board, so you might have to pay 2 coins to other players (per Merchant present in that place!) to get your desired action.  The Mosque tiles are limited to 4, so not all of the 5 players can get each one.

The original short-hop layout can lead to a quick game, since players can jump back and forth between, say, the warehouses and the mosque squares, or the money-generating squares and the Wainwright or the place where you can buy rubies.  If you have more time and are familiar with the game, it's probably better to use one of the longer layouts, or even a random layout.

Tactic to remember: play a $5 card, then move to the Caravansery on the same turn and take it back from the top of the discard pile.

Another tactic to remember, like in worker-placement games: see what other players want, and go there just before they do, to mess up their plans, either by making them pay you $2 for taking the same action, or by taking the Market tile that they were expecting to fulfill.

RATING: 5
While I don't think that this is Rudiger's best outing, I do think that this is a strong offering. Overall, this game is Dorn's trailing tower mechanic but now applied to a light economic worker placement. Resources and money are converted back and forth to meet particular conditions or buy-outs which earn you rubies. First to 5 rubies (or 6 in a two player game) wins. To me, this feels like a crossover between Jambo and his former trailing tower games (i.e., Louis XIV, Traders of Genoa, Goa). Good game, but unfortunately not a stand out either in an ever growing sea of resource-conversion worker-placement games.

RATING: 9
Great racing game, enhanced by its first and second expansions. [-]Haven't tried the second expansion yet, but somehow feel that it might bloat the game a bit.[/-]


It is a fun race, with equal parts strategy and tactics on a variable player board. There are 'light blocks', where opponents have to pay a monetary penalty to the blocker instead of completely blocking out a space. 


The components are excellent: cheerful graphics with solid wooden playing pieces - a far cry from overproduced plastic sculpts and somehow so much more satisfying to the touch.


Update: With both expansions, the Grand Bazaar, this game is a 10, through and through. I have played multiple nights through with only 2 players, something I normally shy away from. It is engaging and the race feels tight, throughout.


Having tried both major expansions, I would never revert to a game without them. Highly recommended!

RATING: 8
JEM 17. szám: http://jemmagazin.hu/magazin/page/2/

Rüdigern Dorn egy újabb nagyszerű játékot alkotott. Isztambul bazárnegyedében mászkálva, kereskedve szert kell tennünk 5 vagy 6 rubinra. Akinek ez sikerül,megnyeri a játékot. Kereskedőként nem kell egyedül tologatnunk a talicskánkat, rendelkezésünkre áll 4 segéd is, akik segítségével kihasználhatjuk a különböző helyszínek adta lehetőségeket. Kis csapatunk együtt indul, a kereskedőt és a segédeket ábrázoló korongok egy tornyot képeznek. Hasonlóan a Genova játékához. 
A játéktáblát 16 helyszínt ábrázoló lapka alkotja
4×4-es elrendezésben, ezeken mozoghatunk a csapatunkkal.
Egy körben legfeljebb két lépést tehetünk az oldalszomszédos lapokra lépve. Ha egy új helyszínre érkezünk, vagy ott kell hagynunk egy segédet, vagy ha már van ott egy, akkor ő ismét csatlakozik kis csapatunkhoz. Ez szükséges ahhoz, hogy végrehajthassuk a helyszín egyedi akcióját.

Nekem a játék kipróbálás után azonnal megtetszett és tudtam, hogy kell egy ilyen a polcomra. Nagyon tetszik benne, hogy több út vezet a győzelemhez, miután a rubinokat több helyen is meg lehet szerezni.

RATING: 6
Two parts of the game are really enjoyable:
(1) Most of the functions of the 16 places are just a  getting/spending resources, but some of them still create fun dynamics. I especially enjoy Tea House.
(2) I admire the family member/governor/smuggler design, which really makes the game shine along with the modular boards.

The one-by-one-ruby-collecting race is a straightforward design, making the heuristic evaluation very clear(i.e. game space is clear & approachable), but also create nothing very surprising & exciting. I appreciate this target-audience based design choice(i.e. family oriented), in spite that it's slightly not my taste. I prefer something more intense.

So it has some very interesting but small dynamics, which are good enough for me to enjoy the game. But lacking some overall climax refrains it from being great.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Great worker placement game with impressively high production value.  The board has beautiful, well illustrated scenes.  Istanbul has well designed movement mechanics with the merchants and their assistants.  There is very little player interaction: when you land on an area with another player's merchant, you must pay 2 lira.  I had a blast playing this game and it was so much fun that we played it twice in the same day.  There are multiple paths to victory.  A huge plus is the way the board is divided into 16 separate rectangular bazaar areas and can be re-arranged differently for each play.  The game's replay value would be much lower with a static board.  The game was exceedingly fun with 4 and 5 players.  I imagine that the game would not be quite as much fun with 2 or 3 players. This game is light enough to introduce to non-gamers, yet it is interesting and not boring and fun.

RATING: 8
The theme of Istanbul doesn't really grab me and the game is not as interesting as the much earlier Rüdiger Dorn games Traders of Genoa and Goa. However, Istanbul delivers a lot of game play in a relatively short time. There are two or three main strategies available and a lot of tension over tactical decisions due to competition between the players for locations. In addition, the random set up provides a fair amount of variety from play to play. With the Mocha & Baksheesh expansion my rating rises to a 9 due to the increase in the available strategies.

RATING: 5.5
Most great euros end just after you've built an engine and had a chance to use it for a short amount of time (the trick being to figure out when to make the switch from engine building to going full out into victory points). 

The engine building in this game is a red herring, the game doesn't last long enough. Instead it's a case of analysing the setup and figuring out in what order to efficiently make a rush for gems.

Even splendor has engine building and that's a quicker game than this.

RATING: 6
Istanbul has very, very, very smooth mechanisms, well represented theme, and good replay value with a modular game board.  That being said, there is little player interaction.  In fact, most often the players spend their time trying to avoid each other.  With everyone focused on their own path across the board, our games had long periods of silence ("Your turn..." "Ok, your turn..."), which I don't consider too fun.  If further proof is needed, the solo variant of the game is exactly the same as the multi-player version.  

This is all a long way of saying, Istanbul is a min-max game.  If you enjoy being able to optimize routes in specific numbers of actions, then Istanbul may be for you.  Otherwise, for as beautiful as the mechanisms are, I traded this game away to get something more social, more interactive.

RATING: 4.5
2014-10-28

Initial Rating: 5.0 (October 2014)

As the Kennerspiel des Jahres winner I was expecting a much better game. It is gorgeous but after your first turn every move is painfully obvious (you can only move 2 spaces), also the downtime with 5 players is brutal as you can´t plan anything on other player’s turns. To add insult to injury, the diamonds (blue goods) and the cards are totally luck driven with almost no way to mitigate them. In my game the player who deserved victory lost because he rolled a 3 and the player who won did so because he rolled a 7+ AND had a card that let him use the Sultan place twice.

Great art and bits. I think its sweet spot is with 3 players, will try it this way sometime in the future. Boring theme for me.

Bottom line, it is a very simple game that didn’t live to its expectation, it has no tension nor interesting decisions. I think it can be a great family game but it was very boring for me. Lots of downtime and I feel luck decides the winner of the game as most decisions are obvious.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I ordered this game because I've always been a fan of Dorn's designs.  When it won the Kennerspiel des Jahres, that was icing on the cake... however, it won't pass Louis XIV as my favourite Dorn design.  

Istanbul is basically a race game.  The first person to gain 5 rubies is the winner.  How you gain those rubies is the core of what is basically a worker placement game with a few twists.

There are lots of hard decisions to be made each turn, and with only 16 places to move to, player interaction is constant.  The luck of the dice for special movement is just enough randomness to add to my enjoyment of the game.

The game isn't as complex as I thought upon first read of the rules, but there are lots of decisions to be made each turn.  My only complaint (which is true of most games with a lot of choices) is that if you tend to play with people who have AP, add 60-90 minutes to the listed play time.  This is way past my optimal play time.  Because of this, I'm only going to give the game a 7.

RATING: 7
Not nearly as tight as some of Dorn's other breadcrumbs games, but much more accessible. While efficiency of actions is still the objective, there are enough elements of chance here that mistakes are not ultimately as damaging. Your strategy will largely focus on the board set-up and leveraging your movement effectively. Other players can get in the way a bit, but it is almost always by chance. Light and enjoyable, but don't expect much depth.

What I like most about this game is that my 12-year-old son and 8-year-old daughter both enjoy it and can play it competitively. It's probably the most advanced game I've played with my daughter and that's exciting. I'll hold onto it until they lose interest. As for playing with adults, I think the interest is already gone.

RATING: 7.5
This has an interesting mechanic of moving around two moves per turn with various helpers that you drop off on the tile where you stop to take an action. You can also pick them up if you visit the space again to take the action again. This leads to trying to find a cycle between tiles in a region. You can also recollect them at one tile. There are some interesting bonus cards which adds to the gameplay. There are two tiles which require dice rolls. This does add some potentially annoying randomness. You gather resources and money and can use both to buy gems which you need to win. I would play it a few more times, but don't see it as becoming a favorite.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I grew warm to this game with more plays stretched over a longer time period after having to play it repeatedly when training for the German boardgame championship. Given the short playtime and comparably easy rules, the luck involved and the imbalance between the methods of getting gems (money is the way to go) is ok.
---
My problem with this game can be traced down to the blue goods and the way to acquire them which is mostly luck-based. Roll high in the black market, run across the smuggler, get the right special card. In one game, I was able to collect the five rubies in only 14 rounds as I got really lucky with rolling high in the black market and delivering in the neighbouring great mosque. The other players all had between one and three rubies.
--- 
In principle, Istanbul is a very simple game. Move one or two steps with your meeple stack, perform an action, done. Where's the beef? Hidden in the 16 different locations players can move to. Hidden is a standard resource collection and conversion game with some engine building. The winner is the player acting most efficiently, i.e. the first to collect five rubies.
I like the very short moves coupled with a decent amount of depth. There's some strategy but tactical considerations are as important. In the end, Istanbul suffers from a lack of innovation. I already couldn't warm to Dorn's coupling of strategic engine building with tactical optimization in Goa and I feel similarly here although it's a completely different game in every other respect.

RATING: 7
This is a great game that I like a ton. I love how you focus on a short term objective, sometimes have to stray away from it for a bit, plan 3-4 moves in advance, but also need a plan B. It's the right balance between planning and screwing up others' plans. I like the multiple paths to victory, and the multiple mini mechanisms: the tea house, the carriage, the markets... The expansion also adds a lot of really interesting stuff, with Guild cards (super powerful, but cost an action), and of course, the coffee resource.

Favorite mechanism: Meetings. The two "NPCs" which give you a bonus if you land on them play a big part in forcing you to adapt, which makes this a much more dynamic game.

RATING: 9.1
This is a strange game when you first teach people. They understand the fact that you drop workers to do an action but they get confused when you tell them that you do the action when they return to their assistants. After a couple of turns they get the notion of you want to go to somewhere but if you won't return there you are not playing in the most efficient way. When they understand they need to find chains to get rubies they really enjoy the game. Every turn matters and you can't waste an action. Some people like to get their cart built and some just go after upgrades before people snag them. I have played many strategies in this and it is still so fresh. You can win by not building up your cart, you can win by ignoring special abilities, you create a lot of distinct strategies and the game rewards that. I love this game so much!

RATING: 6.3
I've enjoyed my couple of plays of this, but I don't feel like I should have. Once the board is set up, there is little randomness, so if you like games with an element of chance, this may not be for you. It seems like a good player could study the board setup at the beginning and basically plan out the whole game.

It's not quite as bad as that, as other players' actions can impact your plans and necessitate course corrections, plus there is a little randomness in dice rolls and card draws.

One element of the mechanics also seems like it should be a big drawback. You have to either drop off or pick up an underling when you move, so if you keep going to new places, you'll run out of minions and start losing time. The greatest efficiency is  to go back and forth over the same ground, so you keep picking up dudes you'd previously dropped off.

Again, in my couple of plays, it wasn't nearly as bad as it sounded, though I expect there are board setups where that would really be an issue.

RATING: 7
Includes the Expansions:
Istanbul: Mocha & Baksheesh (2015)
Istanbul: Letters & Seals (2016)
Istanbul: Pegasus Depot Mini Expansion (2016)

Istanbul, together with the Mocha and Baksheesh expansion, is much better than the base game. The expansion expands the map and offers two new options for gaining rubies. It also adds Guild Cards, which are worth giving up an entire turn in order to play.

The game forces you to think several turns ahead, and even offers you the possibility that your plans will pan out. However, there are many opportunities for your opponents to subtly thwart your plans.

This is one medium weight Eurogame that I have enjoyed quite a bit. In most games, though, there has been a runaway leader who simply couldn't be stopped. That could be a problem.

RATING: 5
Istanbul is the kennersspiel des jahres winner 2014. 
I would be okay it with it winning game of the year as it is fairly light and takes 45 minutes to play.

There are some problems with Istanbul for me and the main problem is that the end requirement (gain 5 rubies, or 6 in a 2P game) is pretty easy to accomplish by all player at the same time. At which tie breaker rules applies.
The tiebreaker rules are pretty odd too. first the money than than the goods and than bonus cards.
I would have preferred a sum of those and probably rubies at 50 + Money + goods time three + bonus cards time two. In case of a tie than the most money, than goods and than possible bonus cards.
Anyway that is besides the point. the point is that with the current victory conditions it boils down to whom has the most money in a lot of cases and for me I think it is a flaw.

RATING: 7.3
Rüdiger Dorn walked a long road to reach 'Kennerspiel des Jahres' status. After more than a decade of Spiel des Jahres and Kennerspiel des Jahres nominations, Rüdiger Dorn got this award for Istanbul which is once again a game he built on a mancala/breadcrumbs mechanism (like in most of his earlier designs). Here he managed making it just as entertaining for "knowing non-gamers" (Kenner - the people who aren't gamers but have already played a few gateways) as it is for gamers, and I guess this feat could not have been accomplished, had he not spent many years designing solely family games a few years ago. So here it is, a race for points, a relatively low complexity mechanism combined with 16 different action spaces, a very good one.

RATING: 6
Starting to tire of this game after 10 plays as it starts to feel quite samey even with changing up the "board".  Still a clever game that can play in an hour, I just don't get excited about it any more.  I've dropped my rating from an 8 to a 7 and wouldn't be surprised if I end up selling this one.  Everyone around me has a copy anyway. Dropped it to a 6 as I can play if others wish, but I won't suggest the game.  Sold my copy.

FIRST IMPRESSION:

I didn't think this was one that I would really consider acquiring, but after my first play I must admit that I really like this one.  The random board setup likely helps as I would otherwise be somewhat suspicious that people would fall into certain paths and the game would start feeling a bit scripted.  Plays quickly, rules are fairly simple, and is a bit of a race game in that the winner is the first person to acquire a certain number of gems, of which there are five places and ways one may do so.  

The mechanic of taking your 3-4 assistants and dropping one off each place you visit and then having to ideally retrace your steps or less ideally, return to home court to regather everyone is interesting and provides an interesting puzzle to work through.  Outside of having to pay $2 when you visit a space with someone else's merchant, there is very little interaction or take that.

RATING: 8
I really liked how "clear" the game is: everything is on the board (or on the small cheat sheet). I really liked the race element created by the small number of points needed for winning (instead of a point salad,…). I liked how going into a specific zone defines part of the tactics *for a "round"*, i.e. until the next meeting at the Fountain: this gives variety. I probably would like the strategies offered by different setups. I also like the relatively legible market for gems, where one can guess the cost in lira of goods. I also like the bonuses: it is always nice to get something sweet, and it spurs me when an opponent gets something nice: I want to run fast and win the race anyway. I liked that the game is not so long (1 small hour?). I liked the fast turns.

The game in itself does not have so much theme (but there is some effort made).

Overall, this is a relatively simple but interesting euro, with builtin, in-game and between-game variety. Nothing earth-shattering, but a solid, enjoyable, "clear" (not complexified) game.

Mir hat auch sehr der zusätzliche Arbeiter gefallen, da man mit ihm das gleiche Feld nochmals aktivieren kann und noch besser ihn in der anderen Ecke des Feldes laufen lassen kann und so Züge noch besser kombinierbar machen kann.
Die Mokka&Bakschisch Erweiterung trägt nun ebenfalls mehr, da man die Plättchen vom Kaffeestand sehr gebrauchen kann um mit den NPCs zu interagieren (von denen es jetzt 4 Stück sind) und nochmehr von dem Zugweite-Plättchen profitiert, da das Brett ja wirklich sehr groß ist.
Was mich sehr gestört hat ist, dass die Felder und die Karten auf 3 Anleitungen verteils erklärt werden. Das ist aber ein Manko, das ich umgehen kann, indem ich einfach selbst Übersichtsblätter erstelle.
Ich hätte echt nicht gedacht, dass mir Istanbul mit so vielem so gut gefällt, aber ausser 2-3 Regeln und einfach mehr Felder kommt ja wirklich nicht dazu.
Eine Erweiterung allein würde ich allerdings nicht mehr spielen. Beide zusammen oder das Grundspiel allein.
Auch der Kebab Shop und der Karanwanenführer Promo sind echt gut integriert und kein nutzloses Beiwerk.

RATING: 7.5
[microbadge=5]

Yet another Rüdiger Dorn let's-trace-a-path game.  This one has some interesting meat to it.  The completely random board setup precludes solving the game quickly, and the penalty for co-occupation kinda forces everyone into different strategies naturally (although at some point, those strategies have to converge to a degree).  This means that every game tends to be fresh.  However, games can still run a little long if the players are even slightly AP prone.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is a smooth game - it plays quickly, it isn't hard to teach or learn, and it is very deep. It is a worker placement, but with some area movement and control - you can move only up to two spaces per turn on a 4x4 grid, so you will have greater or fewer choices depending on where you traveled last turn. Each time you move somewhere, you leave someone behind that you will have to later pick back up (or rest at the fountain to get them all back at once), and if you're visiting a place someone else wants to, they have to pay you for that privilege.

The replayability of this game is exquisite without an insane amount of setup. Each game can be laid out randomly or pre-selected based on the type and kinds of interactions you want, creating games that are different. Since this is a collect and spend kind of game, if the spaces that you need to collect resources are far away from the places you need to spend them, you'll have a lot of people treading on each other and trying to make the best of their long journeys (hopefully encouraging good-natured table-talk). If you have the opposite, then people will separate into their areas and then travel somewhere else when they're done, leaving the previous area behind forever, creating a different kind of game altogether.

The abilities and the cards you can get during the game add a feel of unique powers - each player will probably end up with different abilities, allowing some to move further, others to travel longer between rests, collecting resources more effectively, or cheat on their gambling for better returns. If you're paying attention, the differences become apparent; players will use their signature ability often and will be the only one doing that kind of thing (until someone else manages to collect the same, now-increased-price ability.

RATING: 8
This game deserves all the praise it gets. Although each tile does something different, it's not too hard to figure out and everything is clear after a couple of plays.

The game involves some planning, but the choice usually is between just a couple of optimal moves, so AP should be limited. The board will become quite chaotic (in fact mimicking the hustle and bustle in a real bazaar), leading to some player interaction without any hard take that being involved.

It is probably true that this game can be 'solved', in that experienced players can figure out the optimal paths, even with a custom board lay-out. This is not something the casual gamer will have to worry about though.

RATING: 8
In dem Spiel liegen sechzehn Ortsplättchen (Orte) auf dem Tisch, an denen es etwas zu holen gibt: Waren, Geld, Plättchen für Vergünstigungen und/oder das Objekt der Begierde: Rubine!
Wer am Zug ist, zieht seinen Kaufmann mitsamt Gehilfen einen oder zwei Orte weiter, lässt einen Gehilfen dort oder sammelt einen aus einem vorigen Zug ein und vollzieht so an jedem Ort die dortige Aktion. Das Gesammelte (Geld oder Waren) wird dann z. B. im Sultanspalast oder beim Edelsteinhändler in Rubine umgemünzt. An manchen Orten gibt es zusätzliche Rubine einzusammeln, wenn man bestimmte Voraussetzungen erfüllt.

Games that came to mind when playing; [thing=38992][/thing], [thing=119391][/thing], [thing=70512][/thing] and [thing=1345][/thing].

Plays fast and smooth. It's clear that the theme has nothing to do with the game. Neverteheless, visually the game looks very inviting, with lots of bright colors and beautiful drawings. Unfortunately, not for a second do you think about the beautiful city Istanbul, while playing.

RATING: 9
2017 Top 100 Favorites of All-Time: #14
2016 Top 100 Favorites of All-Time: #45
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
One look at the box cover, and the theme becomes quite obvious. You take on the role of a merchant in Istanbul, trying to buy and sell goods as quick as possible in order to outmaneuver your competition. This commerce takes place in a local bazaar, somewhere in Istanbul. However, there's a slight twist. Instead of trying to collect the most money (lira), you are attempting to gain the most rubies. The first person to gather at least 5 rubies (6 in a 2-player game) wins! Therefore, at its heart, Istanbul is a racing game. 


Theme: It's not just pasted on; rather, the game incorporates the theme into the mechanics. For example, one of the 16 places you can visit is the tea house. Tea is a mainstay of Turkish culture. The history of Turkish tea consumption/trading is rich and well-documented. Accordingly, a tea house can be found in nearly every bazaar. Since you would not typically buy large quantities of tea at the tea house (this would be conducted at a market), the game uses the tea house for a different purpose. Players go to the tea house in order to gamble. They speculate on dice rolls (there are two d6 dice included in the game). Players pick a number between 3 and 12. If the dice roll is equal to or greater than the number that they preselected, they receive lira in that amount. Not only is this REALLY fun to do, it's also historically relevant. Recently, casinos have been banned throughout Istanbul, forcing gambling to become an underground activity. Not to mention the fact that, throughout history, the pub/tea house/coffee house has always been a forum for trade. So, Istanbul not only blends the mechanics with the theme, but keeps it historically accurate. The tea house is used as a case in point, not as an exception. There could be, perhaps, more information on the Istanbul bazaar included in the game. You see this in games by Martin Wallace and Mac Gerdts. Yet, that should not be an expectation, and the absence of it has no effect on actual gameplay. 

Rating: 9.5 out of 10

Components: I'm a components guy, so I am naturally harsh on games that include mediocre components, and very appreciative of those that include great ones. This game falls into the latter category. The modular board pieces (place tiles) are all made of a thick cardboard stock. On a personal note, I HATE paper in board games. Ideally, the only paper should be the instructions. I'm sure that I am in the minority, but I literally won't buy games, despite their good reputation, based on the fact that they have subpar components (Castles of Burgundy and Goa are good examples here). So, the fact that this game has cardboard player boards (your wheelbarrow) and cardboard player aids (double-sided at that), really adds to the overall quality, IMO. Quality of art, of course, is purely subjective. But to me, the art is absolutely beautiful and really adds to the thematic flavor. The plastic ruby pieces are a nice touch. I'm glad they decided not to go with red cubes. The box is sturdy, maybe not as sturdy as the Z-Man production of Robinson Crusoe, but certainly more sturdy that an Alea box. The other bits are standard euro faire, although the stickers for the merchants are a nice touch. The only thing that was missing for me was stickers for the assistants, and maybe some differentiation by player color. 

Rating: 9 out of 10

Gameplay: The game is super smooth, and has great player interaction (although indirect). The point of the game is to gather as much money and goods as possible. Shocker… Once you collect goods, you sell them for money or rubies and you also can convert money directly into rubies. Those familiarities aside, this game offers something I've never seen before. Instead of taking actions yourself, you drop your workers off to do them for you. You essentially manage a team of workers. However, you have to keep a close eye on them. Since they are buying and selling precious goods, they could, at any point, skim some of your profit. As a result, you can never travel very far in this game. Instead of visiting every place in the bazaar, you are naturally limited by geography. You have to create efficient circuits within the bazaar so that you can get everything accomplished, while also keeping your assistants under constant surveillance. These are circuits, not routes. Throughout the game, you are required to retrieve your workers in order for them to buy and sell more goods, so it's not a one-way affair. This is a genius system. Since money in this game is not particularly tight (you can gain money through gambling, selling goods and through bonus cards), the tightness comes in the form of movement restriction. You are not going to be able to do everything. You simply choose a profitable circuit and go about your business. I strongly suggest that you use the preset board for your first play. This initial round will allow you to easily plan your circuit. In addition to circuit building, this game includes dice rolling, pick-up and deliver, and worker placement. The mechanics blend together quite well, and there are certainly many paths to victory. Most importantly, this game showcases a fundamental truth of eurogames in general: rules don't have to be complicated in order for complex decision to occur. In other words, simple rules should be able to lead to rich and complex decision making. 

Rating: 9.5 out of 10


Overall with bias: 9.195 out of 10

RATING: 8
This is a neat game where you race to be the first person to gather a set amount of gems. You can do this many different ways, from buying wagon upgrades, ability tiles to trading goods or money for them. You do this by moving a stack of discs across the tiles, dropping off or picking up a disc in order to activate the tile's action.

I really enjoy this game, it has a variable set-up, where you can adjust between a shorter and longer game, and you can also just do a random set-up as well. It works well regardless of player count.

RATING: 7
Very clever action selection scheme at the core of this game. You can move only one or two spaces and you can only take an action if one of your assistants is already there (then you pick him up), or if you have an assistant with you and agree to leave him on the space.

The rest, i.e., picking up goods, upgrading abilities, and trading for victory points (here: gems) is pretty standard. Nevertheless, the action selection part alone makes this a really good game. Also, I do not think that more elaborate actions would make sense, when you can never freely pick them.

Might pick it up as a "replacement" for Puerto Rico, i.e. to play with people that might enjoy a medium weight game without always having to play PR without expansions with them.

Update: Decided that I will not buy or trade for this. Would gladly play again but do not need to own.

RATING: 8
Istanbul is a great-looking game that plays with 3 - 4 players in less than 1 hour:  making it a fantastic lunch game.  Everyone who I've played it with has loved it.  The main idea of the game is action selection by spatial route movement.  The actions themselves are very straightforward which contributes to the fast paced nature of the game.  The game does not give players a sense of teching up, but of just racing for rubies.

RATING: 9.5
Rudiger Dorn is back! :)
This is a very original and interesting twist on worker placement, fast paced and smart game. The principle of moving around your assistants and having to optimize your paths is very original, but still, the very actions offered on the "board" are also kinda different from a typical worker-placement fair.
A lot of player interaction for an almost-euro (indirect interaction, but you still screw them by using some action before them, so it makes the action more expensive for them... and they need to pay you, if they meet you on the same card), lot of replayability thanks to modular board. Thumbs up and fingers crossed for Spiel des Jahres.

UPDATE: And yes, it won! Kennerspiel des 2014.

RATING: 7.5
Before playing, I was concerned that the game would feel too similar to Ghost Stories -- which we already own -- due to the main mechanic of moving around on a grid of tiles, then taking an action on the tiles. The good news is that the games didn't feel similar.

In fact, the word I'd use to describe Istanbul is pleasant. Not too heavy, not too light, not too stressful, but definitely some interesting decisions to make and an enjoyable experience. I'm surprised the BGG weight of this game is 2.6 -- it felt a bit lighter than that to me. We finished in a brisk 40 minutes -- which I believe isn't uncommon for a 2-player game, but we're both typically slow players so 40 minutes for us is fast!

A pleasant experience overall, and it's nice to have a game that has a little meat on it and which we can play pretty quickly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
To sum up, there are many aspects of Istanbul that I appreciate. The many different options, the balance between long-term strategy and short-term tactical gains, and the subtle interaction that carefully avoid both run-away leader problems and kingmaker problems. The brilliant "worse deal" mechanism also makes the different options easy to compare. Whereas Puerto Rico often sees players counting which goods to ship first to maximize their gains and minimize others' (with downtime as a result), Istanbul immediately shows what deal the next player will get. Yes, Istanbul may be a light-weight game in comparison but you get a lot of game in a short time!

Reviewed at https://boardgamegeek.com/thread/1292671/where-go-istanbul-or-puerto-rico.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
In this 2014 Kennerspiel award winner, one has a merchant and 4 assistants with a wheelbarrow wandering around twelve areas of Istanbul. When you go to an area, you have to drop off an assistant to do the action, but if you go back to the same area where you have an assistant, you can recover the assistant. The different areas allow you to earn cash, goods and tiles, all in an effort to get rubies. First player to five rubies wins the game. 

This is a fast paced game that clocks in under an hour. You are racing under players to get rubies, and you have to be very efficient. It's a fun game, and I'm looking forward to more plays. 

It did win the Kennerspiel, so my expectations were high for this one, perhaps unfairly so. It is a good game, but not sure it deserves the Kennerspiel. There is some interaction with the other players in that you have to pay them $2 if you land on their merchant, and you can interact with other pieces.  It's a pretty compact design and fun to play. There is a bit of luck as well with the dice rolling. Games can be over before you know it, and in this sense, it almost feels like a filler game. There are a few intricacies, but not overly complex to analyze.

RATING: 7
Pretty fun game!

Having to worry about picking up assistants can be distracting, but is still a blast. I love the different ways you can get gems. The cards being laid out randomly can let you form different strategies for every game. I love some of the different areas, like the gambling area, and the jail for you to release your crazy family member. 

Interacting with the sheriff and mayor for extra benefits is also pretty fun. And I love the player's personal cart, where you keep your supplies.

The only issue with this game is it doesn't have the absolute greatest replay value. After enough plays, it'll become a pure test of turn efficiency, which isn't an absolute problem but... it can be.
