RATING: 5
Was expecting a worker placement game and wound up with my first experience with pick-up and deliver gameplay. 

Having actually worked delivery jobs in real life, my friends and I felt Istanbul lacked any sense of urgency. In real life you're always racing a ticking clock and not making it on time can result in the value of the trip being dramatically lessened, or reduced to zero. Istanbul does try to emulate this by several locations having an increasing cost to convert goods or cash into game-winning gems, but the increases are minor and several people can use those locations before really seeing a significant cost for being late to cash in at any one location.

The bizarre drop-off-your-assistants mechanic does create a need for route planning, (another essential delivery skill), to build the necessary supplies for purchasing gems in the fewest possible moves. Unfortunately, the plethora of sources one could obtain gems causes confusion over how best to focus one's efforts and can lead to wildly different scores by endgame.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.9
Certainly, the components are very nice. The illustrations are very nice, the cardboard is thick and once the game is setup, with the merchants and their aides in play, the visuals are fantastic.

The mechanics are catching, but also the game is very fast!! Even if it's your first game, once you bag a few turns, you'll need seconds to do all your turn.

Another feature I loved is that everybody is forced to use different paths, but they all work! The game is more tactic than strategic, you have to adapt to the current state of the board, and you frecuently change your actions because of the last turn of your rivals. Surprisingly, that doesn't slow the flow at all :D

Almost everything in this game is a variable element and the replayability must be infinite.

It's an original and dynamic game, very interesting and likely to have a tense ending.

First impressions [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/148949/istanbul]here[/url].

心得:
競速搶紅寶石的遊戲 
策略性還不錯 
需要事先規劃好重複的路徑 
比想像中好玩
規則:
*起始兩塊錢一張卡，照順位每後一位多一塊
*起始擲骰決定走私販跟市長的位置
*第一個達到結束條件後，每個人還是會執行一樣的回合數，寶石一樣，錢多獲勝
*每回合走1~2步，踩到別人不付錢(2元)，或是沒工人可收放時，就不能動作，水池不用付別人錢
*兩人遊戲時最下面放一個中立小弟token，14,15,16上面各放一個NPC商人
*市長跟走私商都可選擇付兩元或是棄牌跟棄物品來支付拿卡或拿物品的費用
*抓別人小弟可選擇3元或抽一張牌

RATING: 8.5
Rudiger Dorn's favorite component seems to be ever-shifting game boards: they'll move, flip or shift in some way from game to game, providing replayability and a chance to evolve strategies. Louis XIV still reigns as my favorite of his, but Istanbul's design is a bit cleaner and makes it easier to spot opponent's next moves and goals. Definitely his most direct and accessible venture, Istanbul is a light-to-medium weight game, sure to be enjoyed by gamers of any level.

RATING: 7
It works nicely, and is on the upside of a 7, and has interesting Dorn things in it. Move your stack to a spot and do its action, preferably where no one else is so you don't pay a penalty. Accumulate resource capability, accumulate resources, trade in resources for powers and cubes, earn money, trade in money and cubes for VPs. All standard stuff, but done in a nice package. I like how the board changes from game to game, requiring different approaches ... and how your approach will need to change during the game based on what the other players before you are doing, because you just can't afford to keep paying penalties always being second somewhere. That means the game has a strategic and a tactical element, making for constant decision making. Turns still clip along at a decent rate though. There's a little reservation about long term replayability given that you're accumulating the same things in the same way each game, but the race interaction probably gives it enough oomph. Plus there's some card powers/interactions that are worth exploring to see how much weight to give them. Anyway, I enjoyed it and would happily play again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The appeal of this game is its somewhat unique action mechanics and the choices that you must constantly make.  The game has two very nice little mechanics that affect your movement decisions - one is having to make a small payment to other merchants if you land on the space they occupy, and the second is the option of sending a family member to do an action in your place, along with the subsequent action that occurs when other merchants then capture and return your family member.  There is good variety in possible winning strategies, although one must not be passive in the quest for rubies.   For new players the most difficult challenge is mastering the use of one's assistants in a way that you do not lose turns because you run out of assistants.  The best tip for new players is to make sure you originally set the board up using one of the suggested layouts... random layouts can create inequalities that give greater advantages based on turn order or make certain other actions much less desirable.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Istanbul uses Dorn's patented "stack of discs" also found in games like Genoa and Robber Knights. Here, you're moving around a 4 by 4 grid of tiles that you can interact with. You have to leave a disc on each tile you use, giving you a limited time before you have to go back to the Fountain to collect all of your discs. However, if you use a tile you've already used, you can pick up a disc you left there previously instead of leaving a new one, extending your run. So you're incentivized to use the same tiles over and over, but the scoring system really encourages players to use lots of different tiles. You get a ruby (victory point) for obtaining various sets of special abilities, as well as trading in your goods or money in increasingly difficult contracts--first one to a set number wins.

(2 plays)

RATING: 5
Now after several plays over the better part of two years, I'm lowering this one to a 5. Some games are simple but lead to deep strategy after multiple plays. Some games are complex but lead to very little increase in depth of strategy after multiple plays. This is more of a complex/static-strategy game. That being said, it is by no means the worst offender and many popular games are exactly that way. Moreover, this is a fun game. 

As an aside. I'm starting to develop a theory about ways to more quickly identify games that are keepers. One early hypothesis is: "The more time it takes to set a game up if you haven't played it in a while, the less likely the game is to be a keeper." I think this is because some games mistake variability of setup for replayability and have fiddly non-intuitive setup steps that are likely for the purpose of balancing mechanisms. A game of simple rules that allows many strategies will tend to pit players against each other with the game as a conduit for developing and executing strategy. Chess and Go fall into this category, but so do games like BattleLine and Arboretum and Concordia, for instance. A complex game is more like an obstacle course in that the competitors are competing with each other to beat the game the fastest or most efficiently. Some people enjoy this, but I find "obstacle course" games to be more similar to puzzles and inherently less satisfying because they are essentially solitaire games with incidental interaction. People talk about "pasted on theme" but I've never heard anyone talk about "pasted on interaction". Many famous theme-heavy games including Agricola, & LeHavre suffer from this obstacle course / pasted-on-interaction phenomenon. I think it might also have something to do with the correlation between how instinctive the setup is and how flexible and streamlined the mechanisms are. If you find yourself reading the rule book a few times to make sure you've set the game up properly with one fiddly bit over here and three over there and two here or whatever, then it might be an indication that the setup of the game isn't entirely to support gameplay but rather to offset problems with game-play that were identified during play-testing where a fiddly-bit is being used to balance things. The more fiddly bits, the more corner cases being offset, the less streamlined, the less intuitive the setup, the less intuitive the game, the more the game plays you instead of competitors playing each other using the game as a conduit. Anyway. Just the beginning of an inkling for a theory and I'd much rather play Istanbul than LeHavre, and I'd rather play either before Agricola, so YMMV depending on how you feel about those games.

RATING: N/A
P: 2-5, T: 40-60 min, KenSdJ-W
Pick-up and deliver, modular board forming the Grand Bazaar of Istanbul as players race to collect 5 gems. Seems like a solid Euro, and I do think it's thematically satisfying, but still waiting for a p/u&d game to capture my attention (less favorite mechanic). Need reactive, emergent tactics as game unfolds. Dynamics change with player counts. 
Rules are a little bit lengthy for each of the locations (but there is a concise player aid). Game is essentially about path optimization, once you've got it figured out it's hard to be beat (long term replayability concerns). Some press-your-luck dice rolling.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This is a game where you walk across the board, and in each spot you stop you have to drop one of your disks or pick up a disk left on a previous turn to resolve the action. The goal is to be the first to collect 5 rubies, and there are several ways of getting them. Each method increases in difficulty as players snatch them up. There is a LOT of action blocking in this game, as you have to pay other players money to use spots they are sitting on. You also can run out of disks, and need to walk back to the center tile and do a reset action. Every player feels like the player right before them in turn order beats them to what they wanted to do, and they can't afford, or don't want to pay the cost of going to the spot anyway. I want to like it a lot, but I'm not a fan of race to the finish games, and this game can easily have runaway leaders. I also found the degree of action blocking more frustrating than exciting, and the number of things you need to get the next item when the one you were lined up to take is snatched by another player is tedious.

:star: Art and components
:nostar: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:star: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:nostar: Re-playability
:nostar::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment

RATING: 5
Istanbul is the latest Euro to use the faddish modular action grid. I like that the action mostly happens on a shared area, not individual player boards, and I like that it's a simple race to five rather than another tedious spreadsheet totting-up exercise. But the actions (with a couple of exceptions) are still of the pedestrian turn-one-thing-into-another type and the interaction is mostly incidental, making it very hard to pull back a leader.

RATING: 6.5
Feb 2017: Tried it with the coffee expansion and took a lot of the tension out of the game. More ways to gain resources means in this game less opportunities for opponents to get in your way. Now it feels like a game where you pick one strategy right from the start and then go all despite what anyone else is doing. The replayability thus comes from the random board layout rather than what your opponents might do to you, which is typical for many Euros.

March 2015: Worker placement game with a spatial element -- where you can place (or activate) a worker is based on grid movement.

Reminds me a lot of [thing=126163][/thing] as you either place a worker or activate a worker: both this game and Istanbul move very smoothly because there are no separate worker placement and activation phases.

Short and quick-playing. But it's still a Euro engine-building, cube exchanging game, which isn't my sort of thing.
 
Despite the modular board, I don't think there's much replay value; the path to get VPs are the same; the variable board changes the length of these paths.

RATING: 7
Just one play so far, with a full board of five players. I think that, like most worker-placement games, it is probably overly-chaotic with the max number of players, and would improve with fewer people at the table.

Rüdiger Gorn's trademark trail-of-bread-crumbs mechanic is here again, and (as usual) with a nice new twist that I liked. It's an interesting constraint on your movements and choices.

I didn't care for the occasional dice-rolling, but then I usually dislike bet-and-roll mechanisms. Your mileage may vary. At least the dice-rolling is nowhere near being the dominant mechanism; it's just something that crops up in a couple of the worker-placement locations.

I did enjoy the game, and would play again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.49
Fun game.  Great concept of having several 'family members' and to take an action you need to move to a location 1 or 2 away and either drop a family member off or pick one up at that location.  So you don't have the freedom to go anywhere....you need to have a general plan about what you will do.

The set-up takes a little time (putting out the tiles, rubies, tokens, etc...), a few of the rules are a little fiddly, and the icons aren't overly intuitive.

But the game is a good level, a few turns in and a brand new player will completely understand everything going on and what they want/need to do.

Recommended.

RATING: 9
Players: 2-5, best with 4; 40-60 min; Weight: 2.62.
This game playes really well with 2 players.  
Light to medium-light, with a cool theme and artwork. Pawn movement and pick-up and delivery of assistants to utilize buildings in a market (4x4 grid, multiple set-ups possible) to gain resources and money, fulfill orders, and ultimately win by collecting the most rubies. The buildings are fun, e.g. one allows you to "push your luck" by guessing a number, rolling the dice, and getting that many Lira if you roll >= your chosen value. Player interaction is limited to paying opponents if you use a tile they are on, or earning a reward for catching an opponents "family member" and returning them to the jail tile.

RATING: 7
2-5
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[i]I had to practice the game for the German Board Game Championship 2014, which explains my high number of plays. I think Istanbul has a lot of replayability and I would play this again (I cannot say this about the other games I had to practice).[/i]

Fast playing Euro game with a strong spatial aspect. In essence Istanbul is a pick-up-and-deliver game for whole sets of goods. You gather these goods through different actions and either sell these to gain victory points or get money (which you need for other actions and improvements, but also for VP as well). You do your actions by moving your figure through a 4x4-grid of action spaces. 

After 5 actions you have to return to a special space (fountain) to gather back you helpers, unless you visit the same action space more often (which lets you take the helper you left there previously back). When you have planned your way and do a lot of back tracking (i.e. visit places twice), you can avoid losing a turn at the fountain. If you position your figure cleverly on a popular action space your opponents are going to take, they have to pay you some money. The rest of interaction is limited to the race for the different VP, which are getting more and more expensive to get.

The game is a spatial puzzle with limited (planned) interaction and I really liked it. At two places dice rolls decide the effectiveness of your actions, which introduces a small random element (besides cards). My first 4 plays have revealed that different strategies are valid, but also the tactical execution of your strategy and how you react to opportunities created by your opponents will decide over victory and loss. [-]Different setups do not change the game too much, but give rise to different synergies of neighboring action spaces.[/-][i]Update: Actually more plays have revealed that depending on distance between certain places, some strategies are in a specific setup better than others. For experienced players Istanbul shares the following aspect with Dominion: At the start you try to evaluate which strategy will work best in the given setup and then execute it.[/i]
 
The art work is quite nice and the components are fine as well. Actually, the components allow for very elegant play: The way to leave and pick up your helpers is executed by the components and how the prices for the different VP rise are both clever. The rule book is okay, but the description of the bonus cards is too short and lacks some examples (most rule questions will certainly regard these). Since it plays fairly quickly (about 1 hour for a 4-player game), this is ideal to get out as a second game on game night.

RATING: 7
13-07-2014. Typical worker placement game with a fun spatial/route building twist. In Istanbul you race to be the first to acquire 5 rubies. You are a merchant and have aesistants to heop you. Once you take an action, you get to leave guys behind, but do not get them back automatically. So ypu have to go back over them, buy special tiles tiles that allow you to pay for that or return to the start tile (somewhere in the middle). Nice looking boardgame built from tiles to get some flexability and random setup.

RATING: 4
It's good - until you realize it's a single player game and all you do is compare the rounds it took everyone to own 5/6 rubies, which is the game's goal.

Also, rather obvious winning strategy (rushing the red mosque card), giving the starting player a great advantage once everyone got to know the game.

It's still a good game for people who 1) don't like competitive games where your opponents can upset one's plans and 2) don't play often enough or don't want to think too much while playing and are thus not able/interested in seeing the game's all too obvious ever-repeating winning strategy -
in short, what you might call a decent family game.

For more expert players, definitely don't buy it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Doesn't appeal to me very much at all. Everything is so balanced that the end result seems to hang on the luck of someone getting in your way during movement, or which bonus card you picked up. Games always seem to be extremely tight with multiple players finishing on the same number of gems, or being one off. So it always seems to come down to tie breakers. You win or lose and don't feel like you did anything very clever to deserve it, or anything particularly wrong to be denied the win. It's all just very ho hum. No drama. No tension. A bit boring. Then you think that you could have spent the time playing something more fun than micro managing cubes and money. Maybe something with a more exciting theme. (Why is my family member at the police station anyway?)

The movement system seems to have been the inspiration for the game that just melted away in importance. In the more advanced setups it requires a touch more finesse, but still, it's a fairly uninspiring and dull way to steer control.

The only reason I'd play this again is that it's quick. You can bash out a two player game in about 20 minutes and a five player game in under an hour. That's nice, but I've really just got so many better games to play. Some people will really like this sort of light game though, and good on them. Maybe it scratches an itch for some for a simple game at the end of a hard day. I don't see it because you still have to concentrate a bit to do well. Decisions aren't meaningless, just not very fun or interesting.

RATING: 7
Played twice. In Istanbul, players drift their merchants across locations on the board, dropping off or picking up an assistant each time they do so. Players are tested on how efficiently they can spread their pieces out before having to regroup them. That said, I like the feeling of spreading yourself dangerously thin before snapping back to your full self. It's sort of like this quote:

[center][i]“I feel thin, sort of stretched, like butter scraped over too much bread.”[/i][/center]
Ahem. Anyway, the rest of the game is your standard convert-stuff-into-gemstones affair. As the locations can be randomised each game, players will compete in recognising the most efficient routes to victory. While this sounds deadly dull, additional elements of randomly moving non-playable characters (a favourite design flourish of the designer's) and delinquent family members mess around with considerations of where to move to next. Opponents' merchants are also obstacles, so there is some welcome friction as players jostle for coveted locations. 

What I am wary of is the betting minigame at the Tea House. There may be a way to mitigate luck there, but it feels out of place in a game which otherwise promotes control. (One game I played basically came down to a dice roll at the Tea House.) That reservation aside, Istanbul is a good heavy game for the casual crowd. It gets a boost in rating because it moves at a good clip even with the full complement of players.

RATING: 7
The rules are very simple: set the tiles, each showing a different location/power, in some order (3 specific ones included in game, or random). Everyone's stack starts at the Fountain. On your turn, move your stack of tokens (sort of like in Traders of Genoa, but not really: there's a big enough twist to make it feel very fresh). Take the action on the space you land, as long as you either covered your own piece or are able to leave one piece there. Actions give you more goods (4 types), improve your storage capacity, acquire bonus action tiles, trade gems, gamble, etc. If you encounter other players' merchants, pay them. If you encounter the neutral Governor and/or Smuggler tokens (randomly placed at start of game), use them to gain a card and/or cube, respectively, then move them to a new random location. But it's all about acquiring gems - the first player to have 5 wins - and several actions get you those (in various ways: buy them for money, trade gems for them, acquire enough other stuff from the board for them). 

The board, made of a bunch of different tiles, is highly modular for high replayability -- different layouts would change the relationships, optimal routes, etc.

With such a simple rule set, you still get a lot of game play! Turns move along quickly but you have interesting decisions to make almost every time. This seems like a perfect fit for the Kennerspiel des Jahres 2014 ... (update) and it was!
 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a lighter weight action selection/race game played out on a modular board.  Tiles are set up either randomly or following one of the suggested layouts in the rulebook into a grid.  Players have a stack of assistants topped by their merchant and can move 1 or two spaces orthogonally and take the action of the tile they land on.

The movement mechanics in this game are interesting.  Every tile where you take an action you have to leave an assistant behind so your number of available assistants diminishes with each hop to a new tile.  If you don't have an assistant available you don't get to take an action.  However if one of your assistants is already on the target tile, because you had been there in an earlier round, you get to take the action and your assistant back giving you more mileage.

The mechanics are really interesting in this game but I feel like the rest of it is somewhat shallow.  Most of the tiles are some variation on collecting money or goods which are used to buy rubies which are used to win the game.  The board is modular but even with different set ups all the actions you perform are similar enough that this game would probably feel very similar across many plays.  Also, due to the movement mechanics and similarities between the tiles I think players can find action loops that they can just perform over and over to get them to the end game.  This would be boring for both that player and everyone else.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

Istanbul is an efficiency race game with some hidden information and randomness. For the amount of interesting choices it plays pretty fast and has a great moving and action trigger mechanic which rewards planning ahead. There is meaningful player interaction and the game is a very fun puzzle to solve.

Played with both large expansions it gives you several strategic options on how to collect your rubies and lessens the importance of starting player order. For me it’s the best Istanbul experience and my rating is based on it.

Always think about how you should get your next 1 – 2 rubies and make a plan on how to get there with as few actions as possible, make a back-up plan in case of random elements/opponents actions and try to make the most of your bonus cards. If you just do things because the opportunity arises and are not focused enough you will probably fall behind.

RATING: 7.5
Nagyon szépen eltalált felső-kategóriás, családi játék, már-már gémer. A kockadobás első blikkre ijesztően szerencsefaktorosnak tűnik, de a gyakorlatban (pláne a piros mecsetlappal) ez nem érződik. Az egyes fázisok nagyon szépen összeilleszkednek, gyönyörűen felépített a játék. Az első parti után rácsodálkozik az ember, hogy mennyire "szépen össze van rakva".
Folyamatosan taktikázni kell, felmérve a saját és a többi játékos bábuinak aktuális elhelyezkedését. Ugyanakkor, ha nincs stratégia, valami elképzelés, hogy milyen úton-módon szándékszunk rubintokat szerezni, nehéz győzni. Emellett egy harmadik faktor, a jó útvonalak felfedezése, amivel gyorsíthatjuk és tökéletesíthetjük a pénz-/áru-/rubintszerző útvonalainkat. A játék talán legfontosabb pontja ugyanis a megfelelő útvonalak megtalálása.
A mecset lapkák kicsit erősebbek, mint a többi lap. Erre oda kell figyelni, akárcsak a talicskabővítésre: ezek maximális megszerzése az első két parti után kötelezőnek tűnik.
Rengeteg az interakció, de ezeket mégsem érzem agresszívnak, "bántónak" (nincs az a "kiszúrtam veled" élmény).
Jó kis játék ez és ráadásul gyors. A dobozon jelzett 45-60 perc nem hazugság: no ilyet is ritkán tapasztalni...

RATING: 5.5
Average euro, plus some spatial maneuvring element. 

You start out with some workers, some cash and move around the map, getting more cubes, more actions and convert those cubes either directly into points or first into cash and then into points.

Improvement tile that allows you to mess with the dice is clearly the one to get, so you can start dishing in some cash at the Tea-house on your way to the cartmaker or juweler. 

Of course, we only played with the starting setup, and it is bound to change if the setup is changed but to me it holds nothing refreshing. Spatial maneuvring I have seen in Ghost Stories and San Marco f.e. but in reality, would play both games any day over this soulles Euro. Not impressed.

RATING: 7
Ah yes, Rudiger Dorn. Author of a couple of very nice games and even some masterpieces. So when a new title of his hand comes to market, I pay attention.
Dorn's strategy games are usually not on the heavy side, but sorter while still keeping enough depth. And this game is no different. It takes about 45 minutes but has some nice strategic and tactical options and even different paths to explore. All in a nicely developed game system that has a very simple core.
The only negative things I can say is: it doesn't do much new, but what it does it does well. The other thing is that some iconography could have been clearer while other things are missing from the boards, so a couple of extra icons would have been useful.

RATING: 8
Really charming game of action selection and optimization. Fits expertly into the medium weight category. Little to no downtime. It manages to include just the right amount of direct player interaction at the four-player count (haven't played anything else). And the goal is simple: It's a race for gems. I sincerely appreciate its clarity and focus -- a trait missing from so many games these days. My only hesitation is that, even after a single play, I wonder if there is really enough here to warrant digging into the game in anything more than a passing fashion. I hope I'm wrong, and I hope I can maintain my rating even after a few more plays.

RATING: 6
So far, Istanbul seems to be very balanced and to offer numerous legit paths to victory. The movement mechanic is a nifty innovation and the modular board ensures some variation and replayability. Not sure how this will hold up over time, but I've enjoyed my first few plays. Update: Depending on where the Governor and Smuggler end up, Istanbul can become a very lopsided affair (if they tend to stay in the orbit of one person and avoid the areas where others are, victory is all but assured for the lucky player). Even without that, the bloom comes off this rose fairly quickly ... I think in part because after a couple of games. there is no more strategy to explore ... it's just a matter of tactics forcing you down certain paths based on what other players do (given the high cost of following in their footsteps) and how the tiles are set up. I wouldn't say no to playing this now and again, but it's not a title I need taking up shelf space in my collection.

RATING: 5
Incredibly simple, almost gateway-level game, but possibly with a lot more strategy than expected.

On your turn you just make a move then activate an action of the place to where you moved. What adds to the strategy is that you can only move so far before you have to backtrack, and there are sixteen places to go, so there's a ton of options to consider.

Even with all of those options, though, AP doesn't seem to be too big of a problem because of the movement restrictions, so turns tend to be pretty quick and the game has a nice flow to it.

Initial impressions are very good, will have to see how this holds up after more plays.

Update: Seems like there could be a runaway leader issue. If someone gets ahead there's not much anyone else can do to catch up because there is limited interaction. Every game I've played has been anticlimactic as we just play out the final round and watch as the leader gets their final gem to win the game and nobody can do anything to stop it.

Sold.

Have not played enough on different boards to really make a final decision, but the worry is there for me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.75
Istanbul is a eurogame where you're getting resources to convert to money and victory points (gems). There is an interesting movement mechanism, where as the merchant goes to a tile to take the action, it requires an assistant to smooth the way and schmooze the owner. The merchant himself is a bumbling fool who can't manage without an assistant's help.

So if you don't already have an assistant assigned to the tile, you have to leave one behind to take care of things. If you already have an assistant there, he's already put in the time setting things up with that shop and can rejoin you. If you ever end up on a tile without an assistant already there and it's just the merchant, then your incompetent merchant can't do the action and has to end his turn. You can always make your way back to the fountain which allows the merchant to recall all his assistants back to him. Oh and if someone is at the spot you want to go to, you're going to have to pay them to use that spot.

So a large part of the game is figuring out how to get where you want to go and how to manage your assistants. You can only move orthogonally one or two spaces on a turn. And the assistant movement mechanism makes retracing your steps an attractive option if you can find a way to do it and still get a useful action.

The other main part of the game is managing your inventory. You start with the ability to hold two of every good. You can later upgrade your wheelbarrow to hold three, four or five goods of each type. Which you'll want to do because most of the time when you take goods you take all that you can carry. Except jewelry which is harder to get.

Make no bones about it, this is a game where you're trying to solve an optimization puzzle. You've got to figure out the best way of getting gems and the best path through town to do it. If you enjoy that sort of thing, like I do, then this will be an interesting challenge. If you don't, then you probably won't have much fun with this one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
A worthy Kennerspiel winner. Despite us not being massive Eurogame lovers, we like this one.

It's extremely tight with two competitive experienced players, with mild screwage. Like Thurn and Taxis, the first player to make a mistake loses the game. Unlike Thurn & Taxis, the mistake isn't visible from turn two, which reduces the likelihood of early concessions.

The random tile layout increases replayability, and there is strategic variability within the game due to the randomly placed merchants, different strategies (money versus goods), and so on.

RATING: 8
What a nice design. There are some really clever, innovative mechanisms regarding the movement and the actions granted by certain tiles on the board. Lovely game and a great choice for both beginners and experienced players alike. Get the Mocha & Baksheesh expansion as soon as you can! It really opens up the game without adding too much complexity.

The downside of Istanbul is that the setup is really burdensome and although the rules are pretty straightforward it still takes a while to explain how the game works, there is a lot of various tile actions and card abilities. It's funny - although the game is not super complicated, I'm pretty hesitant to teach it to new players. It's kind of a chore. I can explain more complex and involving games in less time. Still, I'll gladly play Istanbul with people who already know the rules. 

By the way - no other game in my collection stresses me out more than Istanbul. I don't know why, but the racing aspect of this game and the constant time pressure almost give me a heart attack every single time I play it.

[b]/update, January 2018[/b]: I've traded it away - I still love it, but it's just too light for my gaming group right now and doesn't hit the table at all. I will most definitely buy it again if my gaming group changes.

RATING: 8.2
Can Teach
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
It’s not often that my wife asks me to pick up a game and it’s even rarer when she teaches it, so I was pretty delighted when both happened with Istanbul. Better yet, I really enjoyed the game!

A quick summary: you have a stack of discs with the top one representing your merchant and the small ones underneath being your assistants. You move around a 4x4 board and you can move 1 to 2 spaces from where you are now. When you move somewhere you take the action of that tile and leave an assistant there. Alternatively, if an assistant is already there you can pick them up and take the action. If you’re out of assistants, you must either go to the fountain or go back to where you’ve placed a previous assistant. The whole while you’re gaining money, goods, and “upgrades” which help you directly and indirectly gain rubies which ultimately decide who wins.

First off, this game does a great job of combatting analysis paralysis. You must move, and you can only go so far. Additionally, if you’ve taken too many actions you must either take an action that’s close enough that has an assistant on it, or you must go to the fountain. In spite of that, your moves and decisions are not pre-determined nor telegraphed, so there’s plenty of strategy to apply here.

I’m happy I own this one now and I’m even happier that it’s a game my wife wanted and taught.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
For all the nice mechanics design the game has big holes in it that forces you to ignore some of the more interesting parts of the game if you actually want to win. This also severely hampers replayibility. Basically you just figure out which 3 spaces you bounce back and forth between to collect coins and go buy your gems. It feels like this wasn't thoroughly playtested. There is a lot to like for a fairly light, fun game. Certainly more interesting than something as silly as Ticket To Ride but it really needs some balancing. It would actually be a bit better if you really had the option of using all the different spaces of the game and still be able to win. Give it a little length and some planning to make you think a bit more. As it stands you just have to identify the shortest route for coin and gem collection and dump your assistants there.

Comments re: Mokka and Baksheesh:
Thoughts after one play - I don't like the expansion. At all. I really, really like Istanbul. I don't care how well i do, i just like playing the game. I didn't enjoy playing with this expansion. But i will play the game a few more times before rating. 

Quick reactions:

The Coffee house ability tile fundamentally undermines the movement aspect of the game, making it far easier to move around to get to locations. 

The guild cards don't seem very balanced at all - some enable you to sell one of each of three resources to get 20 lira, others just let you have 5 coffee, or build a single section of your wagon. The more powerful cards seem overly powerful as well.

I have since integrated all versions together, and I like M&B when combined with B&S - the expansions should have been released the other way around...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
I was first introduced to this game at Gen Con 2014 and was pleasantly surprised. I was even more surprised to find out that I had been playing it wrong for the first handful of times as well but was happy that, not only did what we were doing wrong speed up the play of the game, it also didn't take away anything from the game that I really enjoyed.

The game does fit in to the "gateway game" weight but I don't think that does it justice. The mechanics are simple but there is a lot of strategy. In many ways, you need to be slightly tactical in what you're doing. As everyone is able to see your "wheelbarrow", the number of goods that you're carrying and how close you are to winning the game (e.g., gems), there's a need to be somewhat strategic in how you approach the end of the game. Being able to take advantage of many paths to victory is very beneficial to ensuring that you have options as your opponents block or race for the same objectives.

The ability to change the setup of the game, along with the simple mechanics and game length, makes this game a no-brainer to me. It is an engaging light to medium weight game with a fair amount of strategy in a deceptively simple package.

RATING: 8
Rating based on 1 play. 

Before I played this game I was only mildly interested in it. It looked like a colorful medium-weight euro, but since there are so many of those out there, I didn't pay any particular attention to it (despite its Kennersspiel award).

But then I played it... and I was hooked. This was surprisingly fun! I only played one game and didn't really know what I was doing it, so I ended up dead last, but I found it really interesting and wouldn't have minded to play it once more immediately after that. I feared this would be a game with a lot of AP, but it was barely there, so that was great. The artwork is gorgeous. The fact that you can have a modular board each time you play is fantastic. The mechanic of dropping and picking up your workers worked out a lot better than I'd anticipated.

I'm really looking forward to my next game of Istanbul!

RATING: 7
2-4 players. Bought this blind at Essen because for 24€ and with "Kennerspiel 2014" it should be worth it. So far 1.5 plays. The material is nice and the artwork is pretty. The theme is well done. Overall I like the gameplay, not very complicated to explain except for the movement mechanism. On my first try I had to go to the Well quite a lot to reset my helpers, on the second try I managed to win the game without going to the Well at all. It's a little low on interaction in a two player game. It plays quite fast. A nice, accessible euro game. Not overly complex. Very few random elements (which is a plus). Next time though I'll try the more complicated layout. Oh and the rules don't really explain how to move the smuggler and the governor - the die roll is a sum and there are 16 tiles and they never explain that it's a sum - of course what else can it be. I deduced that some tiles are just not part of the random movement.

RATING: 7
Lots of different ways to approach this; I think Rüdiger Dorn has created another very fun game.  The familiar stacks of mancala-inspired strewing — pretty much trademark Dorn — are not used at all in his usual way; however, as the game progresses, you figure out that they sort of are, but with a little evolution that really is interesting.  I think this game has a lot of replay value, with plenty to explore, especially because the customizable board really discourages strategy ruts, I'd imagine.
